Malebranche</a></li></ul>\n</div></div></div></td>\n</tr><tr><td class="hlist" style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em">\n<div class="NavFrame collapsed" style="border:none;padding:0"><div class="NavHead" 0.3em;font-weight:bold;border-top: 1px solid #aaa; border-bottom: 1px solid #aaa;">\n<p><a href="/wiki/File:046CupolaSPietro.jpg" class="image"><img alt="046CupolaSPietro.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/046CupolaSPietro.jpg/16px-046CupolaSPietro.jpg" width="16" height="12" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/046CupolaSPietro.jpg/24px-046CupolaSPietro.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/046CupolaSPietro.jpg/32px-046CupolaSPietro.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="600" /></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Catholicism" title="Portal:Catholicism">Catholicism&#32;portal</a>\n</p>\n<a href="/wiki/File:Socrates.png" class="image"><img alt="Socrates.png" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Socrates.png/10px-Socrates.png" width="10" height="16" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Socrates.png/15px-Socrates.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Socrates.png/21px-Socrates.png 2x" data-file-width="326" data-file-height="500" /></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Philosophy" title="Portal:Philosophy">Philosophy&#32;portal</a></td></tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Catholic_philosophy" title="Template:Catholic philosophy"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Catholic_philosophy" title="Template talk:Catholic philosophy"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Catholic_philosophy&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>John Duns</b>, commonly called <b>Duns Scotus</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/\xcb\x88/: primary stress follows">\xcb\x88</span><span title="&#39;s&#39; in &#39;sigh&#39;">s</span><span title="&#39;k&#39; in &#39;kind&#39;">k</span><span title="/o\xca\x8a/: &#39;o&#39; in &#39;code&#39;">o\xca\x8a</span><span title="&#39;t&#39; in &#39;tie&#39;">t</span><span title="/\xc9\x99/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;about&#39;">\xc9\x99</span><span title="&#39;s&#39; in &#39;sigh&#39;">s</span></span>, <wbr /><span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/\xcb\x88/: primary stress follows">\xcb\x88</span><span title="&#39;s&#39; in &#39;sigh&#39;">s</span><span title="&#39;k&#39; in &#39;kind&#39;">k</span><span title="/\xc9\x92/: &#39;o&#39; in &#39;body&#39;">\xc9\x92</span><span title="&#39;t&#39; in &#39;tie&#39;">t</span><span title="/\xc9\x99/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;about&#39;">\xc9\x99</span><span title="&#39;s&#39; in &#39;sigh&#39;">s</span></span>/</a></span></span>; <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 1266&#160;\xe2\x80\x93 November 8, 1308), is generally considered to be one of the three most important philosopher-theologians of the <a href="/wiki/High_Middle_Ages" title="High Middle Ages">High Middle Ages</a> (together with <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Aquinas" title="Thomas Aquinas">Thomas Aquinas</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_of_Ockham" title="William of Ockham">William of Ockham</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Scotus has had considerable influence on both <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic</a> and secular thought. The doctrines for which he is best known are the "<a href="/wiki/Univocity_of_being" title="Univocity of being">univocity of being</a>," that existence is the most abstract concept we have, applicable to everything that exists; the <a href="/wiki/Formal_distinction" title="Formal distinction">formal distinction</a>, a way of distinguishing between different aspects of the same thing; and the idea of <a href="/wiki/Haecceity" title="Haecceity">haecceity</a>, the property supposed to be in each individual thing that makes it an individual. Scotus also developed a complex argument for the existence of God, and argued for the <a href="/wiki/Immaculate_Conception" title="Immaculate Conception">Immaculate Conception</a> of <a href="/wiki/Mary_(mother_of_Jesus)" class="mw-redirect" title="Mary (mother of Jesus)">Mary</a>.\n</p><p>Duns Scotus was given the <a href="/wiki/Scholastic_accolades" title="Scholastic accolades">scholastic accolade</a> <i>Doctor Subtilis</i> (<i>Subtle Doctor</i>) for his penetrating and subtle manner of thought. He was <a href="/wiki/Beatification" title="Beatification">beatified</a> by <a href="/wiki/Pope_John_Paul_II" title="Pope John Paul II">Pope John Paul II</a> in 1993.\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#Life"><span class="tocnumber">1</span> <span href="#Metaphysical_argument_for_the_existence_of_God"><span class="tocnumber">4.2</span> <span class="toctext">Metaphysical argument for the existence of God</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-11"><a href="#Illuminationism"><span class="tocnumber">4.3</span> <span class="toctext">Illuminationism</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-12"><a href="#Immaculate_Conception"><span class="tocnumber">4.4</span> <span class="toctext">Immaculate Conception</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-13"><a href="#Veneration"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Veneration</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-14"><a href="#Later_reputation_and_influence"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Later reputation and influence</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-15"><a href="#Later_medieval_period"><span class="tocnumber">6.1</span> <span class="toctext">Later medieval period</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-16"><a href="#Sixteenth_to_nineteenth_centuries"><span class="tocnumber">6.2</span> <span class="toctext">Sixteenth to nineteenth centuries</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-17"><a href="#Twentieth_century"><span class="tocnumber">6.3</span> <span class="toctext">Twentieth century</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-18"><a href="#Bibliography"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Bibliography</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-19"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-20"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-21"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-22"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Life">Life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Duns_Scotus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Duns_Scotus_plaque_University_Church_Oxford.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Duns_Scotus_plaque_University_Church_Oxford.jpg/220px-Duns_Scotus_plaque_University_Church_Oxford.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Duns_Scotus_plaque_University_Church_Oxford.jpg/330px-Duns_Scotus_plaque_University_Church_Oxford.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Duns_Scotus_plaque_University_Church_Oxford.jpg/440px-Duns_Scotus_plaque_University_Church_Oxford.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Duns_Scotus_plaque_University_Church_Oxford.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Plaque commemorating Duns Scotus in the University Church, Oxford</div></div></div>\n<p>Little is known of Duns Scotus apart from his work. His date of birth is thought to have been between 23 December 1265 and 17 March 1266, born into a leading family of the region. The site of his birth, in front of the Pavilion Lodge, near the North Lodge of Duns Castle, is now marked by a <a href="/wiki/Cairn" title="Cairn">cairn</a> which was erected in 1966 by the Franciscan friars of the United Kingdom to mark the 700th anniversary of his birth. Duns Scotus received the <a href="/wiki/Religious_habit" title="Religious habit">religious habit</a> of the Friars Minor at <a href="/wiki/Dumfries" title="Dumfries">Dumfries</a>, where his uncle, Elias Duns, was <a href="/wiki/Custos_(Franciscans)" title="Custos (Franciscans)">guardian</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Duns Scotus\'s age is based on the first certain date for his life, that of his ordination to the <a href="/wiki/Priesthood_(Catholic_Church)" class="mw-redirect" title="Priesthood (Catholic Church)">priesthood</a> at <a href="/wiki/St_Andrew%27s_Priory,_Northampton" title="St Andrew&#39;s Priory, Northampton">St Andrew\'s</a>, <a href="/wiki/Northampton" title="Northampton">Northampton</a>, England on 17 March 1291. The minimum <a href="/wiki/Canon_law_(Catholic_Church)" class="mw-redirect" title="Canon law (Catholic Church)">canonical</a> age for receiving <a href="/wiki/Holy_orders" title="Holy orders">holy orders</a> is 25 and it is generally assumed that he would have been ordained as soon as it was permitted.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> That his contemporaries called him <b>Johannes Duns</b>, after the medieval practice of calling people by their Christian name followed by their place of origin, suggests that he came from <a href="/wiki/Duns" title="Duns">Duns</a>, in Berwickshire, Scotland.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>According to tradition, Duns Scotus was educated at a Franciscan <i><a href="/wiki/Studium_generale" title="Studium generale">studium generale</a></i> (a <a href="/wiki/Medieval_university" title="Medieval university">medieval university</a>), a house behind <a href="/wiki/St_Ebbe%27s_Church,_Oxford" title="St Ebbe&#39;s Church, Oxford">St Ebbe\'s Church, Oxford</a>, in a triangular area enclosed by Pennyfarthing Street and running from <a href="/wiki/St_Aldate%27s,_Oxford" title="St Aldate&#39;s, Oxford">St Aldate\'s</a> to the Castle, the Baley and the old wall,<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> where the Friars Minor had moved when the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Paris" title="University of Paris">University of Paris</a> was dispersed in 1229\xe2\x80\x9330. At that time there would have been about 270 persons living there, of whom about 80 would have been friars.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Duns Scotus appears to have been in <a href="/wiki/Oxford" title="Oxford">Oxford</a> by 1300, as he is listed among a group of friars for whom the <a href="/wiki/Provincial_superior" title="Provincial superior">provincial superior</a> of the English <a href="/wiki/Ecclesiastical_province" title="Ecclesiastical province">ecclesiastical province</a> (which included Scotland) requested faculties from the <a href="/wiki/Bishop_of_Lincoln" title="Bishop of Lincoln">Bishop of Lincoln</a> for the hearing of <a href="/wiki/Confession_(religion)" title="Confession (religion)">confessions</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-CE_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CE-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> He took part in a disputation under the regent master, Philip of <a href="/wiki/Bridlington" title="Bridlington">Bridlington</a> in 1300\xe2\x80\x9301.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> He began lecturing on <a href="/wiki/Peter_Lombard" title="Peter Lombard">Peter Lombard</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Sentences" title="Sentences">Sentences</a></i> at the prestigious University of Paris towards the end of 1302. Later in that academic year, however, he was expelled from the University of Paris for siding with <a href="/wiki/Pope_Boniface_VIII" title="Pope Boniface VIII">Pope Boniface VIII</a> in his feud with King <a href="/wiki/Philip_IV_of_France" title="Philip IV of France">Philip IV of France</a> over the taxation of church property.\n</p><p>Duns Scotus was back in Paris before the end of 1304, probably returning in May. He continued lecturing there until, for reasons that are still mysterious, he was dispatched to the Franciscan <i>studium</i> at <a href="/wiki/Cologne" title="Cologne">Cologne</a>, probably in October 1307. According to the 15th-century writer <a href="/wiki/William_Vorilong" title="William Vorilong">William Vorilong</a>, his departure was sudden and unexpected. He was relaxing or talking with students in the <i>Prato clericorum</i> or <i>Pre-aux-Clercs</i> \xe2\x80\x93 an open area of the <a href="/wiki/Rive_Gauche" title="Rive Gauche">Rive Gauche</a> used by scholars for recreation \xe2\x80\x93 when orders arrived from the Franciscan <a href="/wiki/Minister_General_(Franciscan)" title="Minister General (Franciscan)">Minister General</a>; Scotus left immediately, taking few or no personal belongings.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Duns Scotus died unexpectedly in Cologne in November 1308; the date of his death is traditionally given as 8 November. He is buried in the Church of the Friars Minor there. His <a href="/wiki/Sarcophagus" title="Sarcophagus">sarcophagus</a> bears the Latin inscription:\n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><div class="poem">\n<p><i>Scotia me genuit. Anglia me suscepit. Gallia me docuit. Colonia me tenet.</i><br />(Scotland brought me forth. England sustained me. France taught me. Cologne holds me.)\n</p>\n</div>\n</blockquote>\n<p>The story about Duns Scotus being buried alive, in the absence of his servant who alone knew of his susceptibility to coma, is probably a myth.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> It was reported by <a href="/wiki/Francis_Bacon" title="Francis Bacon">Francis Bacon</a> in his <i>Historia vitae et mortis</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Colophon_(publishing)" title="Colophon (publishing)">colophon</a> of Codex 66 of <a href="/wiki/Merton_College,_Oxford" title="Merton College, Oxford">Merton College, Oxford</a> says that Scotus was also at <a href="/wiki/Cambridge" title="Cambridge">Cambridge</a>, but we do not know for certain if this is true, or, if it was, when he was there.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Work">Work</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Duns_Scotus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Work">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Scotus\'s great work is his commentary on the <i><a href="/wiki/Sentences" title="Sentences">Sentences</a></i> of <a href="/wiki/Peter_Lombard" title="Peter Lombard">Peter Lombard</a>, which contains nearly all the philosophical views and arguments for which he is well known, including the <a href="/wiki/Univocity_of_being" title="Univocity of being">univocity of being</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Formal_distinction" title="Formal distinction">formal distinction</a>, less than numerical unity, individual nature or "thisness" (<a href="/wiki/Haecceity" title="Haecceity">haecceity</a>), his critique of <a href="/wiki/Divine_illumination" title="Divine illumination">illuminationism</a> and his renowned argument for the existence of God. His commentary exists in several versions. The standard version is the <i>Ordinatio</i> (also known as the <i>Opus oxoniense</i>), a revised version of lectures he gave as a bachelor at Oxford. The initial revision was probably begun in the summer of 1300 \xe2\x80\x93 see the remarks in the Prologue, question 2, alluding to the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Wadi_al-Khazandar" title="Battle of Wadi al-Khazandar">Battle of Wadi al-Khazandar</a> in 1299, news of which probably reached Oxford in the summer of 1300. It was still incomplete when Scotus left for Paris in 1302. The original lectures were also transcribed and recently published as the <i>Lectura</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The two other versions of the work are Scotus\'s notes for the Oxford lectures, recently published as the <i>Lectura</i>, the first book of which was probably written in Oxford in the late 1290s,<sup id="cite_ref-Pini_2005_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pini_2005-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> and the <i>Reportatio parisiensis</i> (or <i>Opus parisiense</i>), consisting of transcriptions of the lectures on the <i>Sentences</i> given by Scotus when he was in Paris. A <i>reportatio</i> is a student report or transcription of the original lecture of a master. A version that has been checked by the master himself is known as a <i>reportatio examinata</i>.\n</p><p>By the time of Scotus, these \'commentaries\' on the <i>Sentences</i> were no longer literal commentaries. Instead, <a href="/wiki/Peter_Lombard" title="Peter Lombard">Peter Lombard</a>\'s original text was used as a starting point for highly original discussions on topics of theological or philosophical interest.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> For example, Book II Distinction 2, about the location of angels, is a starting point for a complex discussion about continuous motion, and whether the same thing can be in two different places at the same time (<a href="/wiki/Bilocation" title="Bilocation">bilocation</a>). In the same book, Distinction 3, he uses the question of how angels can be different from one another, given that they have no material bodies, to investigate the difficult question of <a href="/wiki/Individuation" title="Individuation">individuation</a> in general.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:By23_colophonsmaller.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/By23_colophonsmaller.jpg/220px-By23_colophonsmaller.jpg" width="220" height="38" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/By23_colophonsmaller.jpg/330px-By23_colophonsmaller.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/By23_colophonsmaller.jpg/440px-By23_colophonsmaller.jpg 2x" data-file-width="802" data-file-height="139" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:By23_colophonsmaller.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Colophon_(publishing)" title="Colophon (publishing)">Colophon</a> from the edition of Scotus\' <i>Sentences</i> commentary edited by Thomas Penketh (died 1487) and Bartolomeo Bellati (died 1479), printed by Johannes de Colonia and Johannes Manthen, Venice in 1477. It reads <i>Explicit Scriptum super Primum Sententiarum: editum a fratre Johanne Duns: ordinis fratrum minorum</i> Printed versions of scholastic manuscripts became popular in the late fifteenth century.</div></div></div>\n<p>Scotus wrote purely philosophical and logical works at an early stage of his career, consisting of commentaries on Aristotle\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Organon" title="Organon">Organon</a></i>. These are the <i>Questions</i> on <a href="/wiki/Porphyry_(philosopher)" title="Porphyry (philosopher)">Porphyry</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Isagoge" title="Isagoge">Isagoge</a></i> and Aristotle\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Categories_(Aristotle)" title="Categories (Aristotle)">Categories</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/De_Interpretatione" title="De Interpretatione">Peri hermeneias</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Sophistical_Refutations" title="Sophistical Refutations">De sophisticis elenchis</a></i>, probably dating to around 1295.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> His commentary on Aristotle\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Metaphysics_(Aristotle)" title="Metaphysics (Aristotle)">Metaphysics</a></i> was probably written in stages, the first version having started around 1297,<sup id="cite_ref-Pini_2005_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pini_2005-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> with significant additions and amendments possibly after the completion of the main body of the <i>Ordinatio</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> His <i>Expositio</i> on the <i>Metaphysics</i> was lost for centuries but was recently rediscovered and edited by Giorgio Pini.<sup id="cite_ref-StanW_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StanW-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In addition, there are 46 short disputations called <i>Collationes</i>, probably dating from 1300\xe2\x80\x931305; a work in natural theology (<i>De primo principio</i>); and his <i>Quaestiones Quodlibetales</i>, probably dating to Advent 1306 or Lent 1307.\n</p><p>A number of works once believed to have been written by Scotus are now known to have been misattributed. There were already concerns about this within two centuries of his death, when the 16th-century logician <a href="/wiki/Jacobus_Naveros" title="Jacobus Naveros">Jacobus Naveros</a> noted inconsistencies between these texts and his commentary on the <i>Sentences</i>, leading him to doubt whether he had written any logical works at all.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> <i>The Questions on the Prior Analytics</i> (<i>In Librum Priorum Analyticorum Aristotelis Quaestiones</i>) were also discovered to be mistakenly attributed.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> In 1922, <a href="/wiki/Martin_Grabmann" title="Martin Grabmann">Grabmann</a> showed that the logical work <i>De modis significandi</i> was actually by Thomas of Erfurt, a 14th-century logician of the <a href="/wiki/Modistae" title="Modistae">modist school</a>. Thus the claim that <a href="/wiki/Martin_Heidegger" title="Martin Heidegger">Martin Heidegger</a> wrote his <i>Habilitationsschrift</i> on Scotus<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> is only half true, as the second part is actually based on the work by Erfurt.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Metaphysics">Metaphysics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Duns_Scotus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Metaphysics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Realism">Realism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Duns_Scotus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Realism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Scotus is generally considered to be a <a href="/wiki/Philosophical_realism" title="Philosophical realism">realist</a> (as opposed to a <a href="/wiki/Nominalist" class="mw-redirect" title="Nominalist">nominalist</a>) in that he treated universals as real. He attacks a position close to that later defended by <a href="/wiki/William_of_Ockham" title="William of Ockham">Ockham</a>, arguing that things have a common nature \xe2\x80\x93 for example the humanity common to <a href="/wiki/Socrates" title="Socrates">Socrates</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Plutarch" title="Plutarch">Plutarch</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Univocity_of_being">Univocity of being</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Duns_Scotus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Univocity of being">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>He followed <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> in asserting that the subject matter of <a href="/wiki/Metaphysics" title="Metaphysics">metaphysics</a> is "being qua being" (<i>ens inquantum ens</i>). Being in general (<i>ens in communi</i>), as a univocal notion, was for him the first object of the intellect.\n</p><p>The doctrine of the <a href="/wiki/Univocity_of_being" title="Univocity of being">univocity of being</a> implies the denial of any real distinction between <a href="/wiki/Essence" title="Essence">essence</a> and <a href="/wiki/Existence" title="Existence">existence</a>. <a href="/wiki/Aquinas" class="mw-redirect" title="Aquinas">Aquinas</a> had argued that in all finite being (i.e. all except God) the essence of a thing is distinct from its existence. Scotus rejected the distinction. Scotus argued that we cannot conceive of what it is to be something, without conceiving it as existing. We should not make any distinction between whether a thing exists (<i>si est</i>) and what it is (<i>quid est</i>), for we never know whether something exists, unless we have some concept of what we know to exist.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Individuation">Individuation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Duns_Scotus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Individuation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Scotus elaborates a distinct view on <a href="/wiki/Hylomorphism" title="Hylomorphism">hylomorphism</a>, with three important strong theses that differentiate him. He held: 1) that there exists <a href="/wiki/Matter" title="Matter">matter</a> that has no form whatsoever, or prime matter, as the stuff underlying all change, against Aquinas (cf. his <i>Quaestiones in Metaphysicam</i> 7, q. 5; <i>Lectura</i> 2, d. 12, q. un.), 2) that not all created substances are composites of form and matter (cf. <i>Lectura</i> 2, d. 12, q. un., n. 55), that is, that purely spiritual substances do exist, and 3) that one and the same substance can have more than one substantial form \xe2\x80\x93 for instance, humans have at least two substantial forms, the soul and the form of the body (<i>forma corporeitas</i>) (cf. <i>Ordinatio</i> 4, d. 11, q. 3, n. 54). He argued for an original principle of <a href="/wiki/Individuation" title="Individuation">individuation</a> (cf. <i>Ordinatio</i> 2, d. 3, pars 1, qq. 1\xe2\x80\x936), the "<a href="/wiki/Haecceity" title="Haecceity">haecceity</a>" as the ultimate unity of a unique individual (<i>haecceitas</i>, an entity\'s \'thisness\'), as opposed to the common <a href="/wiki/Nature_(philosophy)" title="Nature (philosophy)">nature</a> (<i>natura communis</i>), feature existing in any number of individuals. For Scotus, the axiom stating that only the individual exists is a dominating principle of the understanding of reality. For the apprehension of individuals, an intuitive cognition is required, which gives us the present existence or the non-existence of an individual, as opposed to abstract cognition. Thus the human soul, in its separated state from the body, will be capable of knowing the spiritual intuitively.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Formal_distinction">Formal distinction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Duns_Scotus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Formal distinction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Like other realist philosophers of the period (such as Aquinas and <a href="/wiki/Henry_of_Ghent" title="Henry of Ghent">Henry of Ghent</a>) Scotus recognised the need for an intermediate distinction that was not merely conceptual but not fully real or mind-dependent either. Scotus argued for a <a href="/wiki/Formal_distinction" title="Formal distinction">formal distinction</a> (<i>distinctio formalis a parte rei</i>), which holds between entities which are inseparable and indistinct in reality but whose definitions are not identical. For example, the personal properties of the <a href="/wiki/Trinity" title="Trinity">Trinity</a> are formally distinct from the Divine essence. Similarly, the distinction between the \'thisness\' or <i>haecceity</i> of a thing is intermediate between a real and a conceptual distinction.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> There is also a formal distinction between the divine attributes and the powers of the soul.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Theology">Theology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Duns_Scotus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Theology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Voluntarism">Voluntarism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Duns_Scotus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Voluntarism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Scotus was an Augustinian theologian. He is usually associated with <a href="/wiki/Medieval_theological_voluntarism" class="mw-redirect" title="Medieval theological voluntarism">theological voluntarism</a>, the tendency to emphasize God\'s will and human freedom in all philosophical issues. The main difference between <a href="/wiki/Aquinas" class="mw-redirect" title="Aquinas">Aquinas</a>\'s rational theology and that of Scotus is that Scotus believed certain predicates may be applied univocally&#160;\xe2\x80\x93 with exactly the same meaning&#160;\xe2\x80\x93 to God and creatures, whereas Aquinas insisted that this is impossible and that only analogical predication can be employed, in which a word as applied to God has a meaning different from, although related to, the meaning of that same word as applied to creatures. Duns struggled throughout his works in demonstrating his univocity theory against Aquinas\'s analogy doctrine.\n</p><p>Scotus gave the lecture, <i>Lectura</i> I 39, during 1297\xe2\x80\x931299 to refute the view that everything is necessary and immutable. He claims that the aim of this lecture has two points (<i>Lectura</i> I 39, \xc2\xa731): first, to consider the contingency in what is (<i>de contingentia in entibus</i>); second, to consider how God\'s certain knowledge is compatible with the contingency of things.\nScotus tries to defend the validity of Christian theology against the attack of ancient philosophers. The main argument is unpacked in <i>Lectura</i> I 39, \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa749\xe2\x80\x9353.  Scotus argues that a necessary being (God) is able to have contingent knowledge, and that although this knowledge is contingent, it is not necessarily mutable and temporal by that very fact. \nIn <i>Lectura</i> I 39 \xc2\xa71, Scotus asks, "whether God has determinate knowledge of things according to every aspect of their existence, as according to being in the future."  He presents a counterview which claims that God cannot have determinate knowledge of the future. To support this counterview, Aristotle\'s <i>De Interpretatione</i> IX.\nIn the following arguments, Scotus does not attempt to contradict Aristotle. He does not affirm or reject the ideas of Aristotle. The only issue he argues against is the proposition that God cannot have determinate knowledge of the future. Scotus appears to try to fully demonstrate that Aristotle\'s text is not contradictory to the Christian doctrine of God.\nScotus argues that God wills with one single volition (<i>unica volitione</i>) whatever he wills. God has one volition <i>ad intra</i>, but this one volition can be related to many opposite things <i>ad extra</i>. God can simultaneously will one thing at time 1 and the opposite thing at time 2.\nThere are various possible interpretations of Aristotle\'s <i>De Interpretatione</i> IX.  For example, John Buridan (ca. 1300\xe2\x80\x931362) thought the Scotistic contingency theory was an Aristotelian view.  Buridan\'s judgment is all the more possible because of at least four reasons: (1) Aristotle\'s <i>De Interpretatione</i> IX, 19a23-25 can be interpreted like the Scotistic contingency theory; (2) Scotus himself does not refute Aristotle\'s <i>De Interpretatione</i> IX in <i>Lectura</i> I 39 \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa749\xe2\x80\x9353; (3) Scotus, rather, tries to formulate his contingency theory with the help of other works of Aristotle in <i>Lectura</i> I 39 \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa751, 54; (4) Scotus introduces the diachronic feature of God\'s volition to his contingency theory as well as the synchronic feature.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Metaphysical_argument_for_the_existence_of_God">Metaphysical argument for the existence of God</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Duns_Scotus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Metaphysical argument for the existence of God">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-style ambox-Tone" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/40px-Edit-clear.svg.png" width="40" height="40" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/60px-Edit-clear.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/80px-Edit-clear.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="48" data-file-height="48" /></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section\'s <b>tone or style may not reflect the <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Writing_better_articles#Tone" title="Wikipedia:Writing better articles">encyclopedic tone</a> used on Wikipedia</b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> See Wikipedia\'s <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Writing_better_articles#Tone" title="Wikipedia:Writing better articles">guide to writing better articles</a> for suggestions.</span>  <small><i>(September 2017)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>Duns Scotus argued that it is better to construct a metaphysical argument for the existence of God, rather than the more common physical argument from motion<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> favoured by Aquinas,<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> following Aristotle.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> Though the version in <i>De Primo Principio</i> is the most complete and final version, the <i>Ordinatio</i> proof is usually offered. However, the <i>De Primo</i> version is fascinating and worth looking into for a wider understanding of the argument as well as Scotus\'s metaphysical underpinnings for his argument for God\'s existence, but we shall base this section of the article on the <i>Ordinatio</i> version. Let us briefly outline Scotus\'s argument. He begins his proof by explaining that there are two angles we must take in arguing for the existence of an actually infinite being. First we must approach from the view of the Relative Properties of God; second, from the Absolute Properties of God. Relative properties are those which are predicable of God in relation to creation; absolute properties are those which belong to God whether or not He chose to create. Under the first heading of Relative Properties, Scotus argues for a triple primacy of efficiency, finality and pre-eminence. From there he shows that one primacy implies the others, and finally there can only be one nature that is the First Efficient Cause, Ultimate End, and the Most Perfect Nature. From there the Subtle Doctor discusses the Absolute Properties of God. The First Being is intellectual and volitional, and the intellect and will are identical with the essence of this supreme nature. The First Being is also infinite being. While discussing the infinity of God, Scotus resurrects Anselm\'s argument and responds to the criticism that Anselm makes an illicit leap from concept to reality. Finally, he gives a definite answer of "yes" to the question of whether there exists an actually infinite being. The very next question of the <i>Ordinatio</i> deals with the unicity of the nature thus proved to exist. However, the <i>De Primo Principio</i> version concludes with this argument.\n</p><p>Since the argument is very long and has many parts, we shall content ourselves in this article with stating the premises for the existence of the first efficient cause, assuming that the reader will follow the citations to read the rest of the argument.\n</p><p>\nThe proof for the conclusion that "some efficient cause is simply first such that neither can it be an effect nor can it, by virtue of something other than itself, cause an effect" <i>Ordinatio</i> I.2.43<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> runs something like this:</p><blockquote><p>1)      Something can be produced.</p></blockquote><blockquote><p>2)      It is produced either by itself, nothing, or another.</p></blockquote><blockquote><p>3)      Not by nothing, for nothing causes nothing.</p></blockquote><blockquote><p>4)      Not by itself, for an effect never causes itself.</p></blockquote><blockquote><p>5)      Therefore, by another; call it <i>A</i>.</p></blockquote><blockquote><p>6)      If <i>A</i> is first, then we have reached the conclusion.</p></blockquote><blockquote><p>7)      If <i>A</i> is not first, but also an effect, we return to 2). <i>A</i> is produced either by itself, nothing, or another.  </p></blockquote><blockquote><p>8)      From 3) and 4), we say another, <i>B</i>. The ascending series will either continue infinitely or we finally reach something which has nothing prior to it.</p></blockquote><blockquote><p>9)      An infinite ascending series is impossible.</p></blockquote><blockquote><p>10)    Therefore, etc.</p></blockquote><p>Scotus acknowledges two objections and deals with them accordingly. First is that he begs the question in assuming a first in the series. Here he argues that while many admit an infinite regress in an accidentally ordered series of causes, no philosopher admits infinite regress in an essentially ordered series. Scotus explains the differences between the two and offers proofs for the conclusion that an infinity of essentially ordered causes in a series is impossible.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Second, it is objected that his proof is not really a demonstration since it begins with a contingent premise. That something is produced is contingent and not necessary. Therefore, the proof proceeds from a contingent and not a necessary premise. Scotus says that while that is true, it is utterly manifest that things are produced or effected. But in order to respond, Scotus makes a modal move and reworks the argument. Now he argues from the possibility of production. "It is possible that something can be produced" is a necessary proposition. From there he is able to conclude that it is possible that the first efficient cause exists, and if it is possible that it exists, then it does exist. He assures us that the last claim will be proved later in the argument.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> In the <i>Lectura</i> proof, Scotus argues the following way:</p><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Although beings different from God are actually contingent with respect to their factual existence, nevertheless, they are not with respect to their possible existence. Hence, those entities which are called contingent with respect to their factual existence are necessary with respect to their possible existence \xe2\x80\x93 for instance, although "There exists a man" is contingent, nevertheless "It is possible that he exists" is necessary, because his existence does not include any contradiction. Therefore, "Something \xe2\x80\x93 different from God \xe2\x80\x93 is possible" is necessary, because being is divided into the contingent and the necessary. Just as necessity belongs to a necessary being in virtue of its condition or its quiddity, so possibility belongs to a possible being in virtue of its quiddity. If the first argument is alternatively qualified with the notion of ontological possibility, then we have necessary propositions as follows: It is possible that there is something different from God \xe2\x80\x93 it is not of itself (because then it would not be the case that it were possible), nor from nothing. Therefore, it is possible that it is from something else. Either it is possible that the other agent acts by virtue of itself \xe2\x80\x93 and not by virtue of something else, not being from something else \xe2\x80\x93 or it is not possible. If so, then it is possible that there is a first agent, and if it [is] possible that it exists, then it exists, just as we have proved before. If not and if there is no infinite regress, then the argument at once comes to a standstill.\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>More can and should be said about this fascinating argument, but we leave it to the reader to search out more of the argument. See especially <a rel="nofollow" class="external free" href="http://www.logicmuseum.com/wiki/Authors/Duns_Scotus/Ordinatio/Ordinatio_I/D2/Q2B">http://www.logicmuseum.com/wiki/Authors/Duns_Scotus/Ordinatio/Ordinatio_I/D2/Q2B</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Illuminationism">Illuminationism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Duns_Scotus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Illuminationism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Scotus argued against the version of <a href="/wiki/Illuminationism" title="Illuminationism">illuminationism</a> that had been defended earlier in the century by <a href="/wiki/Henry_of_Ghent" title="Henry of Ghent">Henry of Ghent</a>. In his <i>Ordinatio</i> (I.3.1.4) he argued against the sceptical consequences that Henry claimed would follow from abandoning divine illumination. Scotus argued that if our thinking were fallible in the way Henry had believed, such illumination could not, even in principle, ensure "certain and pure knowledge."<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<dl><dd>When one of those that come together is incompatible with certainty, then certainty cannot be achieved. For just as from one premise that is necessary and one that is contingent nothing follows but a contingent conclusion, so from something certain and something uncertain, coming together in some cognition, no cognition that is certain follows (<i>Ordinatio</i> I.3.1.4 n.221).</dd></dl>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Immaculate_Conception">Immaculate Conception</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Duns_Scotus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Immaculate Conception">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Perhaps the most influential point of Duns Scotus\'s theology was his defense of the <a href="/wiki/Immaculate_Conception" title="Immaculate Conception">Immaculate Conception</a> of <a href="/wiki/Blessed_Virgin_Mary" class="mw-redirect" title="Blessed Virgin Mary">Mary</a> (i.e., that Mary herself was conceived without sin). At the time, there was a great deal of argument about the subject. The general opinion was that it was appropriately deferential to the <a href="/wiki/Mother_of_God" class="mw-redirect" title="Mother of God">Mother of God</a>, but it could not be seen how to resolve the problem that only with <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Christ</a>\'s death would the stain of <a href="/wiki/Original_sin" title="Original sin">original sin</a> be removed. The great philosophers and theologians of the West were divided on the subject (indeed, it appears that even <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Aquinas" title="Thomas Aquinas">Thomas Aquinas</a> sided with those who denied the doctrine, though some <a href="/wiki/Thomism" title="Thomism">Thomists</a> dispute this). The <a href="/wiki/Feast_of_the_Immaculate_Conception" title="Feast of the Immaculate Conception">feast day</a> had existed in the East (though in the East, the feast is just of the Conception of Mary) since the seventh century and had been introduced in several dioceses in the West as well, even though the philosophical basis was lacking. Citing <a href="/wiki/Anselm_of_Canterbury" title="Anselm of Canterbury">Anselm of Canterbury</a>\'s principle, "<i>potuit, decuit, ergo fecit</i>" (He [i.e., God] could do it, it was appropriate, therefore He did it), Duns Scotus devised the following argument: Mary was in need of redemption like all other human beings, but through the merits of Jesus\' <a href="/wiki/Crucifixion" title="Crucifixion">crucifixion</a>, given in advance, she was conceived without the stain of original sin. God could have brought it about (1) that she was never in original sin, (2) she was in sin only for an instant, (3) she was in sin for a period of time, being purged at the last instant. Whichever of these options was most excellent should probably be attributed to Mary.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> This apparently careful statement provoked a storm of opposition at Paris, and suggested the line \'fired France for Mary without spot\' in the famous poem "Duns Scotus\'s Oxford," by <a href="/wiki/Gerard_Manley_Hopkins" title="Gerard Manley Hopkins">Gerard Manley Hopkins</a>.\n</p><p>Scotus\'s argument appears in <a href="/wiki/Pope_Pius_IX" title="Pope Pius IX">Pope Pius IX</a>\'s 1854 declaration of the <a href="/wiki/Dogma" title="Dogma">dogma</a> of the Immaculate Conception, "at the first moment of Her conception, Mary was preserved free from the stain of original sin, in view of the merits of Jesus Christ."<sup id="cite_ref-Life_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Life-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> Scotus\'s position was hailed as "a correct expression of the faith of the Apostles."<sup id="cite_ref-Life_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Life-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Another of Scotus\'s positions also gained official approval of the Roman Catholic Church: his doctrine on the universal primacy of Christ became the underlying rationale for the feast of Christ the King instituted in 1925.<sup id="cite_ref-Life_39-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Life-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>During his pontificate, <a href="/wiki/Pope_John_XXIII" title="Pope John XXIII">Pope John XXIII</a> recommended the reading of Duns Scotus\'s theology to modern theology students.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Veneration">Veneration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Duns_Scotus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Veneration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Long honored as a <a href="/wiki/Beatification" title="Beatification">Blessed</a> by the Order of Friars Minor, as well as in the <a href="/wiki/Archdiocese" class="mw-redirect" title="Archdiocese">Archdioceses</a> of <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Archdiocese_of_St_Andrews_and_Edinburgh" title="Roman Catholic Archdiocese of St Andrews and Edinburgh">Edinburgh</a> and <a href="/wiki/Archdiocese_of_Cologne" class="mw-redirect" title="Archdiocese of Cologne">Cologne</a>, in the 19th-century the process was started seeking his recognition as such by the <a href="/wiki/Holy_See" title="Holy See">Holy See</a>, on the basis of a <i><a href="/wiki/Cult_(religious_practice)" title="Cult (religious practice)">cultus</a> immemorabilis</i>, i.e., one of ancient standing.<sup id="cite_ref-CE_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CE-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> He was declared <a href="/wiki/Venerable" class="mw-redirect" title="Venerable">Venerable</a> by <a href="/wiki/Pope_John_Paul_II" title="Pope John Paul II">Pope John Paul II</a> in 1991, who officially recognized his liturgical cult, effectively <a href="/wiki/Beatification" title="Beatification">beatifying</a> him on 20 March 1993.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_reputation_and_influence">Later reputation and influence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Duns_Scotus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Later reputation and influence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_medieval_period">Later medieval period</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Duns_Scotus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Later medieval period">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Owing to Scotus\'s early and unexpected death, he left behind a large body of work in an unfinished or unedited condition. His students and disciples extensively edited his papers, often confusing them with works by other writers, in many cases leading to misattribution and confused transmission. Most 13th-century Franciscans followed <a href="/wiki/Bonaventure" title="Bonaventure">Bonaventura</a>, but the influence of Scotus (as well as that of his arch-rival <a href="/wiki/William_of_Ockham" title="William of Ockham">William of Ockham</a>) spread in the fourteenth century. Franciscan theologians in the late Middle Ages were thus divided between so-called Scotists and Ockhamists. Fourteenth century followers included <a href="/wiki/Francis_of_Mayrone" title="Francis of Mayrone">Francis of Mayrone</a> (died 1325), <a href="/wiki/Antonius_Andreas" title="Antonius Andreas">Antonius Andreas</a> (died 1320), <a href="/wiki/William_of_Alnwick" title="William of Alnwick">William of Alnwick</a> (died 1333), and John of Bassolis (died 1347), supposedly Scotus\'s favourite student.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sixteenth_to_nineteenth_centuries">Sixteenth to nineteenth centuries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Duns_Scotus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Sixteenth to nineteenth centuries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>His reputation suffered during the <a href="/wiki/English_reformation" class="mw-redirect" title="English reformation">English reformation</a>, probably due to its association with the Franciscans. In a letter to <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Cromwell" title="Thomas Cromwell">Thomas Cromwell</a> about his visit to Oxford in 1535, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Layton" title="Richard Layton">Richard Layton</a> described how he saw the court of New College full of pages from Scotus\'s work, "the wind blowing them into every corner."<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/John_Leland_(antiquary)" title="John Leland (antiquary)">John Leland</a> described the Oxford Greyfriar\'s library in 1538 (just prior to its dissolution) as an accumulation of \'cobwebs, moths and bookworms.\'<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>When in the sixteenth century the Scotists argued against <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_humanism" title="Renaissance humanism">Renaissance humanism</a>, the term <i>duns</i> or <i><a href="/wiki/Dunce" title="Dunce">dunce</a></i> became, in the mouths of the Protestants, a term of abuse and a <a href="/wiki/Synonym" title="Synonym">synonym</a> for one incapable of <a href="/wiki/Scholarly_method" title="Scholarly method">scholarship</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Despite this, Scotism grew in Catholic Europe. Scotus\'s works were collected into many editions, particularly in the late fifteenth century with the advent of <a href="/wiki/Printing" title="Printing">printing</a>. His school was probably at the height of its popularity at the beginning of the seventeenth century; during the sixteenth and the seventeenth centuries there were even special Scotist chairs, e.g. at Paris, Rome, Coimbra, Salamanca, Alcal\xc3\xa1, Padua, and Pavia. New ideas were included <a href="/wiki/Pseudographical" class="mw-redirect" title="Pseudographical">pseudographically</a> in later editions of his work, such as the <a href="/wiki/Principle_of_explosion" title="Principle of explosion">principle of explosion</a>, now attributed to <b>Pseudo-Scotus</b>.  Scotism flourished well into the seventeenth century, and its influence can be seen in such writers as <a href="/wiki/Descartes" class="mw-redirect" title="Descartes">Descartes</a> and Bramhall. Interest dwindled in the eighteenth century, and the revival of scholastic philosophy, known as <a href="/wiki/Neo-Scholasticism" class="mw-redirect" title="Neo-Scholasticism">neo-Scholasticism</a>, was essentially a revival of Thomistic thinking.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Twentieth_century">Twentieth century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Duns_Scotus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Twentieth century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The twentieth century saw a resurgence of interest in Scotus, with a range of assessments of his thought.\n</p><p>For one thing, Scotus has received interest from secular philosophers such as Peter King, Gyula Klima, Paul Vincent Spade, and others.\n</p><p>For some today, Scotus is one of the most important <a href="/wiki/Franciscan" class="mw-redirect" title="Franciscan">Franciscan</a> theologians and the founder of <a href="/wiki/Scotism" title="Scotism">Scotism</a>, a special form of <a href="/wiki/Scholasticism" title="Scholasticism">Scholasticism</a>. He came out of the Old Franciscan School, to which <a href="/wiki/Haymo_of_Faversham" title="Haymo of Faversham">Haymo of Faversham</a> (died 1244), <a href="/wiki/Alexander_of_Hales" title="Alexander of Hales">Alexander of Hales</a> (died 1245), <a href="/wiki/John_of_Rupella" class="mw-redirect" title="John of Rupella">John of Rupella</a> (died 1245), <a href="/wiki/William_of_Melitona" title="William of Melitona">William of Melitona</a> (died 1260), St. <a href="/wiki/Bonaventure" title="Bonaventure">Bonaventure</a> (died 1274), <a href="/wiki/Cardinal_(Catholicism)" class="mw-redirect" title="Cardinal (Catholicism)">Cardinal</a> <a href="/wiki/Matthew_of_Aquasparta" title="Matthew of Aquasparta">Matthew of Aquasparta</a> (died 1289), <a href="/wiki/John_Peckham" title="John Peckham">John Peckham</a>, Archbishop of Canterbury (died 1292), <a href="/wiki/Richard_of_Middletown" class="mw-redirect" title="Richard of Middletown">Richard of Middletown</a> (died c. 1300), etc., belonged. He was known as "Doctor Subtilis" because of the subtle distinctions and nuances of his thinking. Later philosophers in the sixteenth century were less complimentary about his work and accused him of <a href="/wiki/Sophism" class="mw-redirect" title="Sophism">sophistry</a>. This led to the word "<a href="/wiki/Dunce" title="Dunce">dunce</a>," which developed from the name "Dunse" given to his followers in the 1500s, becoming used for "somebody who is incapable of <a href="/wiki/Scholarly_method" title="Scholarly method">scholarship</a>."\n</p><p>An important question since the 1960s has revolved over whether Scotus\'s thought heralded a change in thinking on the nature of \'being,\' a change which marked a shift from Aquinas and other previous thinkers; this question has been particularly significant in recent years because it has come to be seen as a debate over the origins of \'modernity.\' This line of argument first emerged in the 1960s among popular French philosophers who, in passing, singled out Duns Scotus as the figure whose theory of univocal being changed an earlier approach which Aquinas had shared with his predecessors.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> Then, in 1990, the historian of philosophy Jean-Francois Courtine argued that, between the time of Aquinas in the mid-thirteenth century and <a href="/wiki/Francisco_Su%C3%A1rez" title="Francisco Su\xc3\xa1rez">Francisco Su\xc3\xa1rez</a> at the turn of the seventeenth, a fundamentally new approach to being was developed, with Scotus taking a major part in its development.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> During the 1990s, various scholars extended this argument to locate Scotus as the first thinker who succumbed to what <a href="/wiki/Martin_Heidegger" title="Martin Heidegger">Heidegger</a> termed \'onto-theology\'. \n</p><p>In recent years, this criticism of Scotus has become disseminated in particular through the writings of the \'Radical Orthodox\' group of theologians, centred around <a href="/wiki/John_Milbank" title="John Milbank">John Milbank</a> and <a href="/wiki/Catherine_Pickstock" title="Catherine Pickstock">Catherine Pickstock</a>. The Radical Orthodox model has been questioned by <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Horan" title="Daniel Horan">Daniel Horan</a><sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> and Thomas Williams<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>, both of whom claim that Scotus\' doctrine of the univocity of being is a semantic, rather than an ontological theory. Both thinkers cite Ord. 1, d. 3, pars 1, q. 3, n. 163, in which Scotus claims that "This [univocally] is how all the authoritative passages one might find on this topic in the Metaphysics or Physics should be inter- preted: in terms of the ontological diversity of those things to which the concept is attributed, which is compatible with there being one concept that can be abstracted from them". Such a quotation seems to refer to epistemology, with abstracted concepts, rather than with ontology, which Scotus admits can be diverse.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Bibliography">Bibliography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Duns_Scotus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Bibliography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<dl><dt>Works in rough chronological order</dt></dl>\n<ul><li>Before 1295:\n<ul><li><i>Parva logicalia</i>\n<ul><li><i>Quaestiones super Porphyrii Isagogem</i></li>\n<li><i>Quaestiones in librum Praedicamentorum</i></li>\n<li><i>Quaestiones in I et II librum Perihermeneias</i></li>\n<li><i>Octo quaestiones in duos libros Perihermeneias</i></li>\n<li><i>Quaestiones in libros Elenchorum</i></li></ul></li></ul></li>\n<li><i>Quaestiones super libros De anima</i> (1295\xe2\x80\x931298?)</li>\n<li><i>Quaestiones super libros Metaphysicorum Aristotelis</i> (1298\xe2\x80\x931300?; Longpre</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-27"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-27">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><i>Die Kategorien- und Bedeutungslehre des Duns Scotus</i> ("Duns Scotus\' Theory of the Categories and of Meaning", 1915)</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-28"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-28">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><i>Opus Oxoniense</i> I iii 1\xe2\x80\x932, quoted in Grenz 2005, <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=VjHTcrWmguQC&amp;lpg=PP1&amp;pg=PA55#v=onepage&amp;q&amp;f=false">p. 55</a></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-29"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-29">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Honderich p. 209</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-30"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-30">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation journal">Woo, B. Hoon (2016). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.academia.edu/30598029/_The_Difference_between_Scotus_and_Turretin_in_Their_Formulation_of_the_Doctrine_of_Freedom_Westminster_Theological_Journal_78_no._2_2016_249-69">"The Difference between Scotus and Turretin in Their Formulation of the Doctrine of Freedom"</a>. <i>Westminster Theological Journal</i>. <b>78</b>: 258\xe2\x80\x9362.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Westminster+Theological+Journal&amp;rft.atitle=The+Difference+between+Scotus+and+Turretin+in+Their+Formulation+of+the+Doctrine+of+Freedom&amp;rft.volume=78&amp;rft.pages=258-62&amp;rft.date=2016&amp;rft.aulast=Woo&amp;rft.aufirst=B.+Hoon&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.academia.edu%2F30598029%2F_The_Difference_between_Scotus_and_Turretin_in_Their_Formulation_of_the_Doctrine_of_Freedom_Westminster_Theological_Journal_78_no._2_2016_249-69&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ADuns+Scotus" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446"/></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-31"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-31">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Lectura I, d. 2, q. 2, 40 Now efficiency can be considered either as a metaphysical or as a physical property. The metaphysical property is more extensive than the physical for "to give existence to another" is of broader scope than "to give existence by way of movement or change." And even if all existence were given in the latter fashion, the notion of the one is still not that of the other. It is not efficiency as a physical attribute, however, but efficiency as the metaphysician considers it that provides a more effective way of proving God\'s existence, for there are more attributes in metaphysics than in physics whereby the existence of God can be established. It can be shown, for example, from "composition and simplicity," from "act and potency," from "one and many," from those features which are properties of being. Wherefore, if you find one extreme of the disjunction imperfectly realized in a creature, you conclude that the alternate, the perfect extreme exists in God.\nAverro\xc3\xabs, therefore, in attacking Avicenna at the end of Bk. I of the Physics, 14 is incorrect when he claims that to prove that God exists is the job of the physicist alone, because this can be established only by way of motion, and in no other way \xe2\x80\x93 as if metaphysics began with a conclusion which was not evident in itself, but needed to be proved in physics (For Averro\xc3\xabs asserts this falsehood at the end of the first book of the Physics). In point of fact, however, [God\'s existence] can be shown more truly and in a greater variety of ways by means of those metaphysical attributes which characterize being. The proof lies in this that the first efficient cause imparts not merely this fluid existence [called motion] but existence in an unqualified sense, which is still more perfect and widespread.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-32"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-32">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Summa Contra Gentiles, I, 13 <a rel="nofollow" class="external free" href="http://dhspriory.org/thomas/ContraGentiles1.htm#13">http://dhspriory.org/thomas/ContraGentiles1.htm#13</a></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-33"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-33">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"We shall first set forth the arguments by which Aristotle proceeds to prove that God exists." SCG I, 13.2  <a rel="nofollow" class="external free" href="http://dhspriory.org/thomas/ContraGentiles1.htm#13">http://dhspriory.org/thomas/ContraGentiles1.htm#13</a></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-34"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-34">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text"
